PT J
AU Blecha, I
AF Blecha, I
TI Phenomenology and the critique of pluralist reason
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB Post-modern philosophy has propounded the idea that reality is founded primarily on plurality and dissent and that contemporary thought must come to terms with this situation. One moderate representative of the post-moderns, Wolfgang Welsch, sees the future of new forms of rationality in the so-called transversal reason, which will be able to both take the necessary steps towards dissenting discourses, and at the same time to take its essence and variety into account. The article compares this position with the phenomenological defence of the existence of the ''natural world" as a single horizon for the course of human existence and for inter-subjective understanding, and it asks how far this view is in conflict with the post-modern argument of dissent. It sees the principal difference as lying in the form of the moderns' critique: while the postmoderns criticise modern rationalism as the construction of a single world and argue for the "construction" of more worlds, phenomenology sees the principal problem of the moderns in the technique of "construction", i.e. in the inability to move beyond the demands of pure subjectivity and think of reality as something essentially "other". For phenomenology the ''natural world" is the horizon of the unrevealed, the potential the non-actual. This world can be "one" without this excluding the possibility of many different approaches to it.
C1 Univ Palackeho, Filosoficka Fak, Olomouc, Czech Republic.
RP Blecha, I (reprint author), Univ Palackeho, Filosoficka Fak, Olomouc, Czech Republic.

PT J
AU Rombach, H
AF Rombach, H
TI The tao of phenomenology
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB Rombach's article is an attempt at a new understanding of phenomenology. After the initial stage of phenomenological thought with Husserl's transcendental phenomenology (characterised by the a priori position of transcendental subjectivity as an anonymous system for the active life of the consciousness) and through Heidgger's attempts at a critique of the assumption of any transcendental position from the standpoint of a fundamental-ontological phenomenology (characterised by hermeneutic reality and the experience of forgetfulness of being), Rombach proposed a step towards a graduated structure of phenomenology. This transition is based on Rombach's critique of Heidegger's analysis of the structure of human being, which is not understood in the entirety of its actual state but rather from the position of a very controversial interpretation of the history of European thought. The main idea behind Rombach's proposal is a stress on the phenomenon of changes in the very basic structure of human being in individual historical upheavals: the actual state of human being is the basis of history and of life. Man and nature both arise from one basic process, from the genesis of essence and being at the same time. This basis and the founding genesis of the structure of being for being are the subject of phenomenology in its new guise; this is a phenomenology of both the deep and superficial phenomena of both this event and its structural unit. Rombach sees this path to arousing human consciousness of itself, of its place in the whole, of the constituent events in individuals and in societies, and of the force and laws of human historical existence, as the real "Tao" of (phenomenological) thought.

PT J
AU Nitta, Y
AF Nitta, Y
TI The east-west phenomenon
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The article looks at the phenomenality of thought as something which, in the eastern intellectual tradition, emerges with the phenomenonalisation of the world. The author begins with three Ideas: 1) consciousness as the original state of the world, 2) the changing of ideas through the negation of the I in confrontation with the invisible and immaterial dimension, 3) the basis of knowledge lying in the difference between variety (plurality) and identity (the individual). On this basis he investigates the concept of the phenomenon and the self-destructive intellectual path in Mahaja Buddhism and for Nishida, who played an important role in the reception of European culture. He tries to find a parallel between Nishida and western thought (Husserl, Heidegger and Fink) in which he sees new possibilities for phenomenological thought. In his conclusion he considers philosophy as self-observation of life and the phenomenology of events.

PT J
AU Louzil, J
AF Louzil, J
TI Bernard Bolzano - Apologist or heretic?
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB In 1805 Bernard Bolzano (1781-1848) was appointed to the newly-established chair of religious studies at the philosophical faculty of the Charles University in Prague. His appointment was clearly intended in the spirit of the Austrian restoration under Chancellor Metternich: for the defence and restoration of the Catholic-Christian faith among the future intelligentsia. Although Bolzano made determined attempts to achieve this aim, in January 1820 he was removed from the chair after repeated investigations of his reported failure in his pastoral and civic responsibilities. He was accused of a number of political and dogmatic doubts, although his most serious deviation from official church doctrine, i.e. his philosophical justification of the highest moral law as objective truth - independent of God and his will - that is of truth as such, was surprisingly lacking from the list. The article gives a concise history of this concept and traces the response to it from Bolzano's contemporaries and from later thinkers. Bolzano was undoubtedly a devout Christian and so an ardent apologist of (reformed) catholicism. His concept of the highest moral law was nevertheless in clear conflict with church teachings and Bolzano was a more or less conscious heretic. This fact is of course only interesting from a cultural history point of view today. Bolzano's thinking on the sphere "of self" did, on the other hand, have considerable influence and lasting significance for the subsequent development of philosophy (on the modern theories of objects, values and intentions, and on Husserl's phenomenology).

PT J
AU Sobotka, W
AF Sobotka, W
TI Reinhold, Hegel and enquiry into "constellations" (On the influence of
   K.L. Reinhold on the introductory essay in Hegel's The Science of Logic,
   "With What Must Science Begin")
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB This article, drawing on Henrich's method of "constellations", inquires into the influence of Reinhold on Hegel's essay With What Must Science Begin appearing at the start of his The Science of Logic. Hegel's original negative reaction to Reinhold in the work The Difference between Fichte's and Schelling's System of Philosophy was based on a failure to grasp the onto-theological nature of Reinhold's theory and also on the thesis, that philosophical science as an objective totality is self-justifying and does not require any further justification.
   Later in the The Phenomenology of Spirit and in The Science of Logic, Hegel takes phenomenology as the basis of logic, that is of pure knowledge. The introductory essay to With What Must Sicence Begin includes a methodological justification of the new conception, which found one of its sources of inspiration in the methodological considerations of K. L. Reinhold. Hegel kept to this position later when he no longer considered The Phenomenology to be the first part of philosophical science. The article deals in detail with the little-recognised position that Hegel had towards Reinhold's methodological considerations and with the differences between both Version of Hegel's essay in the first and second editions of The Science of logic.

PT J
AU Park, BC
AF Park, BC
TI Wittgenstein's use of the word 'Aspekt'
SO SYNTHESE
LA English
DT Article
AB Wittgenstein frequently uses the word 'aspect' (Aspekt) in his writings from 1947 to 1949. There he uses the word along with aspect-seeing and aspect-change, so that readers are misled into thinking his primary concern in using the word is something like Gestalt psychology or philosophy of psychology per se. However, Wittgenstein's late treatment of aspect is only a special case of a more general problem, namely phenomenology. In the middle-period writings, the word 'aspect' refers to a phenomenological object. Basically, Wittgenstein's aspect means the way an object appears to us. For him, an 'aspect' is a phenomenological object.
C1 Pusan Univ Foreign Studies, Dept Philosophy, Pusan 608738, South Korea.
RP Park, BC (reprint author), Pusan Univ Foreign Studies, Dept Philosophy, Pusan 608738, South Korea.

PT J
AU McIntosh, D
AF McIntosh, D
TI Husserl, Weber, Freud, and the method of the human sciences
SO PHILOSOPHY OF THE SOCIAL SCIENCES
LA English
DT Article
ID CRITIQUE
AB In the debate between the natural science and the phenomenological or hermeneutical approaches in the human sciences, a third alternative described by Husserl has been widely ignored. Contrary to frequent assumptions, Husserl believed that a purely phenomenological method is not generally the appropriate approach for the empirical human sciences. Rather, he held that although they can and should make important use of phenomenological analysis, such sciences should take their basic stance in the ''natural attitude,'' the ordinary commonsense lifeworld mode of understanding which cuts across the divergent abstractive specializations of natural science and phenomenology. Human science in the natural attitude, shorn of its naivete by phenomenological insight, would be the field of descriptive concrete sociocultural sciences capable of taking a truly explanatory approach to their subject matter, persons and personal formations. In practice, both Weber and Freud exemplify the method recommended by Husserl.

PT J
AU Beyer, C
AF Beyer, C
TI Hussearle's representationalism and the "hypothesis of the background"
SO SYNTHESE
LA English
DT Article
AB John Searle's "hypothesis of the Background" seems to conflict with his initial representationalism according to which each Intentional state contains a particular content that determines its conditions of satisfaction. In Section I of this essay I expose Searle's initial theory of Intentionality and relate it to Edmund Husserl's earlier phenomenology. In Section II I make it clear that Searle's introduction of the notion of Network, though indispensable, does not, by itself, force us to modify that initial theory. However, a comparison of this notion to the notion of "horizon" from Husserl's later phenomenology and an interpretation of Husserl's conception of the "determinable X" as providing a solution to the problem of perceptual misidentification lead me to conclude that in his discussion of 'twin examples' Searle had better modified his initial theory. Finaily, I critically examine Searle's claim that "anyone who tries seriously to follow out the threads in the Network" will eventually reach a "bedrock" of non-Intentional capacities. In Section III I show in detail, partly in a rather Husserlian vein, that Searle's four official arguments for the Background thesis, though containing some very valuable contributions to a theory of linguistic skills, are not convincing at all if they are to be understood as going beyond the scope of(Hus)Searle's 'content-cum-Network' picture of Intentionality. The upshot of these considerations is that the Background thesis should be read as a thesis concerning the causal neurophysiological preconditions of human Intentionality rather than concerning the logical properties of Intentional states in general. Recently Searle himself has come to the same result, but he does not say for which reasons. The present essay makes it clear why Searle just had to arrive at this important result.

PT J
AU Cassedy, S
AF Cassedy, S
TI Gustav Shpet and phenomenology in an orthodox key
SO STUDIES IN EAST EUROPEAN THOUGHT
LA English
DT Article
DE Gustav Gustavovich Shpet; Edmund Husserl; phenomenology; Russian
   religious thought; philosophy of language
AB Gustav Gustavovich Shpet (1879-1937) is undoubtedly best known for introducing Husserlian phenomenology to Russia. He applied to aesthetics and the philosophy of language the principles he had discovered in Husserl's Logical Investigations and Ideas I. But, perhaps without knowing it, he modified the phenomenology he had found in Husserl. His modifications show a thinker who is thoroughly grounded in Russian religious thought of the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries. The result is a philosophy that combines Husserl's analysis of the structure of consciousness with the fundamental Platonism of Orthodoxy, the doctrine of incarnation, and the related notion that matter is to be venerated.

AU Rota, GC
AF Rota, GC
TI The phenomenology of mathematical beauty
SO SYNTHESE
LA English
DT Article; Proceedings Paper
CT Symposium on Proof and Progress in Mathematics at the 1995-96 Program of
   the Boston Colloquium for Philosophy of Science
CY FEB   12, 1996
CL BOSTON UNIV, BOSTON, MA
SP Boston Univ, Ctr Philos & Hist Sci
HO BOSTON UNIV
AB It has been observed that whereas painters and musicians are likely to be embarrassed by references to the beauty in their work, mathematicians instead like to engage in discussions of the beauty of mathematics. Professional artists are more likely to stress the technical rather than the aesthetic aspects of their work. Mathematicians, instead, are fond of passing judgment on the beauty of their favored pieces of mathematics. Even a cursory observation shows that the characteristics of mathematical beauty are at variance with those of artistic beauty. For example, courses in ''art appreciation'' are fairly common; it is however unthinkable to find any ''mathematical beauty appreciation'' courses taught anywhere. The purpose of the present paper is to try to uncover the sense of the term ''beauty'' as it is currently used by mathematicians.
RP Rota, GC (reprint author), MIT,DEPT MATH,CAMBRIDGE,MA 02139, USA.

TI Husserl's phenomenology and Weyl's predictivism
SO SYNTHESE
LA English
DT Article
AB In this paper I discuss the version of predicative analysis put forward by Hermann Weyl in Das Kontinuum. I try to establish how much of the underlying motivation for Weyl's position may be due to his acceptance of a phenomenological philosophical perspective. More specifically, I analyze Weyl's philosophical ideas in connexion with the work of Husserl, in particular Logische Untersuchungen and Ideen I.
   I believe that this interpretation of Weyl can clarify the views on mathematical existence and mathematical intuition which are implicit in Das Kontinuum.

TI Emergence and deconstruction - The development of important
   relationships of moments of temporality and reflection in Hegel,
   Heidegger and Derrida
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB Hegel saw the process of negation as drawing subjects into itself, thus overcoming their formerly positive nature and leaving them to function in their mutual otherness. Hegel's idea of dialectic disturbance was the inspiration for Gadamer's concept of experience as detypification.
   Heidegger's ontology of time criticised both the reflective philosophy of consciousness and Husserl's phenomenology as subordinating our receptiveness to the sign of being which is already open to us.
   Jacques Derrida moved beyond his own semiotic philosophy of pre-Kantian and ahistorical moments. His concept of the generation of meaningful and deeply temporal levels of the process of denomination gives concrete form to Merleau-Ponty's "meaning at the moment of birth".

AU Kohak, E
AF Kohak, E
TI Phenomenology and ecology - Support and interdependence
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The author claims that in their original intent as a call to life in responsibility, phenomenology (Husserl's Wiener Vor tr ag) and ecology (Hans Jonas in Das Prinzip Verantwortung) support each other. However, in their romantic form (Heidegger's Die Frage nach der Technik, John Seed et al., Thinking like a Mountain) they form a co-dependency. Author defines romanticism as a quest for a return to primordial innocence which fails in (romantic) ecology's attempt to return to nature but draws another lease on life from (romantic) phenomenology's quest for innocence in an inner nature. Author expresses the hope that the experience of human ability to destroy nature (nuclear explosion, population explosion, consumption explosion) might yet awaken humankind from dreams of innocence regained to responsibility accepted, vindicating phenomenology and ecology in their original intent.

TI A rigorous thinker in irreconcilable times
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The author considers the wider relevance of Patocka's thought, taking into account not only his philosophical theses but also their foundation in his personality and life. He concentrates primarily on Patocka's early texts in which he speaks of "philosophical heroism", and tries to show that while Patocka's thought did not achieve a fundamental synthesis, it became more obviously "existentially rooted" in a single tendency, on a path that went: beyond the early "heroism" with both its basis in the practical sphere (which is a fundamental moment in Patocka's teaching on the three movements of human existence) and in its inter-subjectivity of intentionality.
   The author concludes that the phenomena with which Patocka was concerned in the last years of his life show that the everyday was increasingly important in his philosophy, which was inspired by Husserl and Heidegger, being a realm of action and responsibility and as the only place in which what the author terms the "phenomenology of shaken thought" can be understood, something which is an essential counterpart of Patocka's civic activism.

PT J
AU Ricoeur, P
AF Ricoeur, P
TI Jan Patocka: From a philosophy of the natural world to a philosophy of
   history
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The article seeks Patocka's work in the form of an ellipse with its focal points in the phenomenology of the natural world coming from Husserl's Crisis and Heidegger's Being and Time, and in the question of the meaning of history. The former of these can be found in the Papiers phenomenologiques (Phenomenological Documents) of 1965-1975 and in Prirozeny svet jako filosoficky problem (The Natural World as a Philosophical Problem). The main writings around the second starting point are the Kacirske eseje o filosofu dejin (Heretical Essays) written in the last years of Patocka's life. The author seeks to show that the relation between these two points is not arbitrary but that they are instead fundamentally interrelated: on the one hand the world we live in is the natural world only in relation to what scientific knowledge brings to it, but - like the experience of the world - it is also a cultural world. On the other hand there is the origin of the world, that which the history of the world calls the "prehistoric" world. In both of these there is something that precedes it in both pre-scientific and prehistorical terms, which is in some sense its foundation.

PT J
AU Rabanus, C
AF Rabanus, C
TI The meaning of history in the "heretical essays"
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The essay at hand deals with Jan Patocka's philosophy of history as it is recorded in his Heretical Essays Developing against the background of the problem of the natural world and thus locating Patocka's philosophy between those of Edmund Husserl and Martin Heidegger, this examination will centre around the concept of "history" and of "meaning" and around the problem of the meaning of history. With reference to Patocka's theory of the three movements of human existence and to his dividing history into three epochs, his concept of "history in its actual meaning" shall be explained. In this way it will be found that this concept of history indicates a certain epoch, namely that epoch in the development of human Being in which man has become conscious of his freedom and of his responsibility. The examination of Patocka's concept of meaning then supports the thesis that "meaning" with Patocka is the same as "horizon" in the philosophy of Husserl, that "meaning" is "what makes the understanding of a thing possible". As a consequence, only man's Being can have meaning. This result is afterwards related to Patocka's concept of an a-subjective phenomenology which then opens the question of the origin of "meaning" and finally leads to the question of the Appearing-in-itself The last part of the essay outlines the power and also the limits of Patocka's a-subjective phenomenology.

PT J
AU Blaschek-Hahnova, H
AF Blaschek-Hahnova, H
TI The sphere of phenomena and structural events
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The present article takes up again to the essay The Meaning of History in the "Heretical Essays" by Christian Rabanus. In it Patocka's further development of Husserls phenomenology is contrasted in several ways with the approach of Heinrich Rombach, beginning with his Strukturontologie. "Sense" is regarded as a category of structure formation. The article also tries to illustrate that Patocka's concept of a "a-subjective phenomenology" offers little help in overcoming subjectism, of wich Husserl is accused by both autors. Patocka isn't able to liberate himself from the dichotomy "subjective" vs. "a-subjective" and therefore remains tied to a certain type of subjective philosophy. Rombach's structural categories "dimensionality" and "con-creativity" seem to offer better possibilities for a radical break in thinking about reality, employing many different "ontological tableaus". The "new subjectivity", which Husserl an his two successor search, becomes more evident in applying Rombach's approach. Nevertheless it is possible to make Patocka's approach useful by appying Rombach's method of "up-interpretion" developed in his Philosophische Hermetik, a follow-up work of Strukturontologie.
C1 Goethe Inst, Praha, Czech Republic.
RP Blaschek-Hahnova, H (reprint author), Goethe Inst, Praha, Czech Republic.

PT J
AU Rodrigo, P
AF Rodrigo, P
TI Jan Patocka, plate and phenomenology
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The author of this article starts out from Patocka's lectures on Plate and Europe. Europe, its history and its metaphysical fate formed the crux to which Patocka's hermeneutics constantly return, since for him philosophy was closely linked with the philosophy of the situation. He brought together Plate's call for care for the soul with Husserl's instruction to "go to the heart of things".
   At the beginning of his lectures on Plate and Europe Patocka looked at the concept of the situation. In the case of contemporary Europe he was primarily concerned with its loss of a spiritual base. As Europeans try to understand the original meaning hidden in the variety of events, they move away from their basic role as a "being of truth".
   Patocka sees the antique view of man as a being of truth as playing a fundamental role in Plate's thought and in his description of Socrates, of whose existence Patocka was firmly convinced. Patocka rejected Zeller's thesis of the difference between the picture of Socrates in Plate's early dialogues and in his more mature ones and argues that all of them dealt with the care for the soul.
   If this is so, it is necessary to agree with Patocka that the most real basis of contemporary Europe is the idea first expressed by Plate and later adopted by the phenomenologists that the soul is not res cogitans, but rather existence.

RP Rodrigo, P (reprint author), Univ Montpellier 3, F-34032 Montpellier, France.

PT J
AU Richir, M
AF Richir, M
TI The possibility and necessity of subjective phenomenology.
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB This study is a detailed analysis of Patocka's outline of "a-subjective phenomenology", which was intended as a step away from Husserl's phenomenology. Marc Richir aims to indicate the possible directions in which this outline could have developed. Starting from Patocka's critique of Husserl's cogito - cogitatio - cogitatum, which implies the transfer of evidence from the phenomenological sphere to the apparently subjective one, and the reduction of the horizontal aspect of events to the meaning described in the cogito, so a transfer of the phenomenological field (in the postulate of transcendental consciousness) to the sphere of subjectivity. The author joins Patocka in suggesting a different road than this "subjectivism", seeking the possibility of an ontological-existential meaning of phenomena. The horizontal aspect can be translated (in Heideggerian terms) as an outline of all possible encounters with the essence which is related to that essence of possibilities, which is in some way possible, which incorporates the sum which is left out of Husserl's schemata.
   Richir then links this part of Patocka's thought with his concept of phenomenology (stressing the importance of Merleau-Ponty and his attempt to found an ontology rooted in the phenomenon of the body and corporeality). An a-subjective phenomenology would see events, means and styles as incarnate existences. As well as, for example, implying a differing concept of temporality, this would mean a reconsideration of the phenomenological understandings of eidos (a "conceptless eidetics", e.f. Richir's work Phenomenes, temps et etres, 1987) and also a deeper understanding of the phenomenality of phenomena which is based in phenomenological ontology.

PT J
AU Sobotka, M
AF Sobotka, M
TI Patocka's 1949 lecture on Hegel's "phenomenology of mind"
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The article analyses Patocka's unfinished lecture on Hegel's Phenomenology of Mind given in the 1948-1949 summer semester. Patocka wished to give a systematic outline of the whole Phenomenology, but in the available time was only able to reach the chapter Die Verwirklichung des vernunftigen Selbstbewusstseins (The Realisation of Rational Self-Consciousness). The manuscript of the lecture, which is held in the Jan Patocka archive, is therefore only a torso, but it is nevertheless a very interesting text which shows the influence of the French existentialists, Kojeve and Hyppolite. Patocka also used the interpretation of W. Dilthey. In the lecture Patocka emphasises the motif of finished man as a destroying-crating being. Patocka wished to read the Phenomenology as philosophical anthropology and as a philosophy of history in which the sense is not determined from the beginning, but which is created in the dramatic circumstances of history.

PT J
AU Kouba, P
AF Kouba, P
TI The necessity of crisis
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article; Proceedings Paper
CT Conference on Phenomenology in Present-Day Europe
CY NOV, 1995
CL UNIV TRIESTE, TRIESTE, ITALY
HO UNIV TRIESTE
AB This article considers the changing meaning of crisis in phenomenology. It begins with Husserl's concept of crisis, which saw it as arising out of the inconsistency of European culture, and set aside the theme of ''forgotten'' intentional achievement of subjectivity. This opened the way for a fully demonstrated theoretical truth.
   Heidegger criticised Husseri for his faith in the possibility of objectivising the world as a whole, and showed that intentional structures are embedded in the way we exist. Heidegger initially saw this transcending way of being, i.e. ec-static temporality, both as being-in-the-world, i.e in those things with which wt: come into contact, and as stepping outside ourselves, self-loss, which implies the possibility of a ''return'' to authentic being with oneself.
   Patocka rook up this second aspect and developed Husserl's ideas further: in overcoming crisis and the consistent self-realising of reason it is not enough to rely on apodictic theoretical evidence, which requires the practical-moral achievement of authentic being (care for the soul).
   Heidegger later moved away from the possibility of such a foundation in the resolve of the subject (and so from the possibility of ''overcoming crisis'') and turned to an expectant idea of being itself, although he did not return to the idea of being-in-the-world.
   The author shows that the concept of crisis can also be understood in a positive way. It then becomes obvious that being-in-the-world is necessarily being-in-crisis, that crisis is an appeal to which we must respond. The task of philosophy is not to look for some ''general answer'' which eliminates crisis; it should take us ''deeper'' into crisis, forcing us to exist in all its aspects, i.e. ''to be more''.
RP Kouba, P (reprint author), CTR TEORET STUDIA,PRAGUE,CZECH REPUBLIC.

AU Blecha, I
AF Blecha, I
TI Eugene Fink's concept of the world
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The passage of Eugene Fink, one of Edmund Husserl's best-known students, from a strenuous defense of his mentor's ideas to a severe criticism of them is an interesting one. The article traces the motives and results of this about-rum. It concentrates on both the concept of ''world'' which is central to Fink's philosophy, and on identifying the fundamental features of Fink's confrontation with Husserl and with phenomenology itself. In this confrontation the dual parameters of time and space in Fink's world, which furnish the basic conditions for the ontic delimitation of all beings, were shown to be a very schematic structure in which existence is only given, prior and external, not related to its nature as a whole. In the same way, it is difficult for a person to step into such a world and it seems to represent a symbolic intervention in the game. Such a rethinking of the essential traditional motives of Husserl's phenomenology may be worthwhile, especially insofar as the phenomenality of phenomena is linked to the horizon of the co-given, which is a certain condition for the manifestation and delimitation of any existence. Fink's distinction between these also seems possible and this leads to reflection on basic metaphysical problems, showing that phenomenology has much to offer.
RP Blecha, I (reprint author), UNIV PALACKEHO,FILOSOFICKA FAK,OLOMOUC,CZECH REPUBLIC.

AU Major, L
AF Major, L
TI Patocka's phenomenology of meaning and Heidegger
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB Patocka's late non-subjective phenomenology is well known. One of its important aspects is not properly analysed up to this time - i.e. the outline of phenomenology of meaning.
   It can be explained by the fact that the demand to elaborate the real philosophy of meaning was pronounced in his late essay About Masaryk's Philosophy of Religion (1976). But some basic ideas of his own theory were mentioned sooner.
   In the above mentioned essay about Masaryk, Patocka deals very thoroughly with Kant's conception of meaning of human life. Kant's solution is according to him wrong, because it puts together meaning with purpose, i.e. with causality. The notion of 3 causality is however only ontic, while the notion of meaning must be understood as ontological.
   The right conception according to Patocka is not explained either by Kant and the metaphysics before him, or by their opponents (for ex. Nietzsche). it is no doubt that the first ones understand the necessity of the total and absolute meaning, but they imagine it as something eternally being, non-problematically given. The second ones are only satisfied with the relative and partial meaning.
   Heidegger's fundamental ontology opens in accordance with Patocka the way to the right solution. Patocka is inspired by Heidegger, but at the same time he remains very critical. He follows especially Heidegger's existential care (Serge) as the unity of three ecstases (Ekstase) of temporality (Zeitlichkeit). Patocka deals with three basic existentional movements of human life, each of them has its own specific time character. The movement of acceptation is linked with past, the movement of reproduction is linked with present and finally the movement of truth is linked with future. The manner how to understand the total meaning of our life and world depends on the fact what movement or time ecstasis we prefer. Only the third one enables the real authentic understanding. It makes for the liberty and self-finding of man in the devoted duty on the others, to the highest possible interhuman communication.
   At the end of his life, Patocka analysed the problem of meaning in the close connection with existential mood of anguish, with phenomenon of human finally and nothing. In accordance with Patocka, being is nothing existing, but it makes its last and absolute sense to everything existing.
RP Major, L (reprint author), CHARLES UNIV,FAK FILOSOFICKA,PRAGUE,CZECH REPUBLIC.

AU Bayerova, M
AF Bayerova, M
TI Husserl's phenomenology and its relation to Descartes
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The influence of Descartes on Husserl's phenomenology stretches back to the first appearance of phenomenological thought as transcendental philosophy, but became clear only in the later stage of Husserl's work where it acquired its special value as the historicising aspect of philosophical thought. The logic of the development of phenomenology up to this final phase with respect to Descartes, can be seen primarily in following three main points. The first, in the very start of the shaping of phenomenology's theoretical-understanding role and methods in close connection with Descartes' ideas an doubt in Husserl's Idea of Phenomenology. The second, in the legacy of Husserl's work Ideas I which is an application of Cartesian thought, being at the same time a scheme of questions inspired by Descartes and successfully superimposed upon it. The third, in Husserl's attempt at a systematic introduction to phenomenology in his Cartesian meditations. Here his criticism of Descartes' errors reveals the unending field of transcendental subjectivity for universal phenomenological description, in which was clarified, among other things, the concept of evidence and where the possibility of practical knowledge in the transcendental field was understood as a mode of evident - making.
   Husserl's efforts to fill out the idea of transcendental subjectivity, for which he found the historical source in Descartes, and his final attempt to lay out the pre-scientific and extra-scientific worlds of our life became central to the broader philosophical discussion of the 1960s. Here too, thanks to Husserl's final philosophical work, a new appreciation of his overall oeuvre also in relation to Heidegger appeared, Characteristic evaluative approach, which is outlined in the conclusion to this article, underlines the fact that Husserl's consistent upholding of a phenomenological position in such new areas as the understanding of the world in all its universality opened new possibilities to explain phenomenology's link with Descartes.
RP Bayerova, M (reprint author), AV CR,FILOSOFICKY USTAV,PRAGUE,CZECH REPUBLIC.

PT J
AU Major, L
AF Major, L
TI Descartes in the connection with Husserl's and Patocka's phenomenology
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB In Cartesian Meditations Husserl evaluates the contemporary situation of philosophy as a deeply decadent one. The crisis according to him can be warded off only by the development of strictly scientic transcendental phenomenological philosophy. The contemporary phenomenology struggles for linking to cartesian philosophy, to become the neo-cartesianism. It means strictly to differentiate what is alive in Decartes' philosophy, permanently valued and on the contrary what is mistaken.
   Descartes is according to Husserl an author of two significant steps. Firstly, he renewed the demand that philosophy must be a universal science from the absolute evidence. Secondly, he discovered this absolute evidence in the turn to subject and that's why he initiated the passage from the naive objectivism to the transcendental subjectivism. But simultaneously Descartes committed two basic errors, depreciating his important contribution. Instead of the transcendental subjectivism he created a nonseless transcendental realism.
   The first Descartes' error was the fact that he uncritically identified the idea of science with a deductive and purely formal and universal mathematics. The second one represented the fact ''epokhe'' of the world was not performed consequently, ego cogito remained for him as ''the last rest of world''. This necessitated to Descartes' understanding of ego, subject as substantia cogitans - i.e. thing sui generis. Descartes' theory of knowledge according to Husserl is nonseless in principle, because it presupposes what ought to be proved.
   In the late Crisis of European Sciences Husserl is not interesting in neo-cartesianism. He tries to overcome the deep spiritual crisis of Europe. That is Husserl, who tries to present Descartes as a decided and simultaneously deeply contradictory person of European spiritual history. Descartes, according to Husserl, following G. Galilei initiated paradoxically two mutually antagonistic directions of modern thinking - mathematical objectivism on one hand and transcendental subjectivism on the other. It is possible to say that Husserl's present interpretations of Descartes' philosophy are less constructed and exact that the former ones.
   The study of Descartes' philosophy has a good tradition in the Czech Republic. Jan Patocka is nevertheless the best expert. His interest in Descartes is influenced by acceptation of phenomenology. He was occupied with Descartes as historically as in systematical context. His first works are historical and they are inspired by Husserl's interpretation. They accent the revolutionary originality of Descartes' philosophy and its inner contradiction. In the well-known book Natural World as a philosophical Problem Patocka presents that there are also two cogito at Descartes, i.e. cogito cogitans and cogito cogitatum.
   In the 60ies Patocka returned to Descartes. In his book Aristotle, His Predecessors and Heirs he analyzed very originally the basic intelectual connection between Descartes and Hegel. In his late university lectures, inspired strongly by Heidegger, Patocka analyzed Descartes very interestingly in the connection with the problematics of the subjective body. Patocka evaluated the significant Descartes' contribution in the fact that he appeared the problematic of subjective body, unfortunately he could not exploit it.
RP Major, L (reprint author), UNIV KARLOVA,FILOSOFICKA FAK,PRAGUE,CZECH REPUBLIC.

AU Seifert, J
AF Seifert, J
TI Philosophy as an exact science
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB As the title reveals, the author tries to sketch the main contributions of the realistic phenomenology to the new foundation of the classical realistic philosophy. In the present paper, he traces his own philosophical position as worked out in detail in his recently published papers on the background of a critical dialogue with the idea of philosophy as pure science which was developed by the father of phenomenology Edmund Husserl (esp. Husserl's Logos article Philosophie als strenge Wissenschaft). The author chooses some of the most important theses of Husserl's article and examines them critically.
   Therefore Seifert regards the answering of the above Kantian question as a significant, if not revolutionary contribution of the realistic phenomenology to the foundation of a strictly scientifical philosophy. A carefully elaborated datum of the substantial necessity as a true primordial fact accessible to our mind, of its ontological foundation, as well as of its accurate distinction from other essentially different kinds of necessity makes it possible, according to Seifert, to get critically over the scepticism and relativism, both of them being immanent in the Kantian philosophy. At the same time, the foundation of the a priori cognition and of the synthetical one in the objective and absolute substantial necessity overcomes the more radical immanentism and subjectivism of the later Husserl. Hence such a philosophy of substantial necessity and the simultaneous rediscovery of the esse as real being represent a substantial contribution of the realistic phenomenology to the new foundation of the scientifical philosophy, a contribution that we owe to the realistic phenomenologists, namely Reinach and Hildebrand.

PT J
AU ZNOJ, M
AF ZNOJ, M
TI HEGEL AND OUR DEMOCRACY
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The article lays out the main topics of the debate between liberalism and communitarianism on the field of political philosophy and considers the extent to which the Czech tradition of Hegelian consideration can play a part in this debate. The analysis of Milan Sobotka's latest work devoted to Hegel's Phenomenology of Mind and Philosophy of Law concludes that Sobotka, following the analysis of Joachim Ritter, succeeds in interpreting Hegel's concept of morality and his critique of Kantian morality in a way which accepts the key themes of the debate between liberalism and communitarianism, removing Hegel from the neomarxist field.

PT J
AU Stroker, E
AF Stroker, E
TI Intentionality - The fundamental problem of Husserl's philosophy
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The present paper (read on the 19th November 1992 at the Philosophical Faculty of Charles' University in Prague) traces in a methodically consequent way the basic categories of Husserlian phenomenology which are bound with remarkable changes of the central phenomenological notion - the intentionality. By means of these categories as expression of the above mentioned changes, the approach as well the meditation of the evolutional stages of the basic structure and basic sense of Husserl's phenomenology as a whole are acquiered. The stages are as follows: descriptive phenomenology of acts and finally transcendental phenomenology.

PT J
AU SOBOTKA, M
AF SOBOTKA, M
TI WOMIT-MUSS-DER-ANFANG-DER-WISSENSCHAFT-GEMACHT-WERDEN - OUTLINE OF THE
   PROBLEM OF THE BEGINNING OF THE PHILOSOPHY FROM DESCARTES TO HEGEL
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The author begins from the thesis that it was modern philosophy that first recognised the problem of the beginning in the systematic sense.  Descartes, who transformed metaphysics into a ''discipline of the principles of human understanding'', recognised two beginnings:  one is cogito-sum as a simple statement which is the first thing for us, and the second is the truth of God as the ontological source of all truth.  With this Descartes created the paradigma of ''metaphysical knowledge'' (Metaphysik der Erkenntnis) which dominated European philosophy after Kant.
   The author then turns to the problem of the beginning in German idealism.  While Fichte saw the ''myself'' as the absolute basis of philosophy, Schelling and Hegel moved on from this view from the self with reference to the ontological anchoring of the unity of perception and being.  In Hegel's The Phenomenology of Mind on the one hand and in the Science of Logic on the other, the concept of two beginnings in the sense of knowing what is first for us and what is first in itself once again appears.  The move of consciousness from sense experience to absolute knowledge is conditioned by the ontoteological constitution of knowledge.  It is a concealed shift of knowledge (in the ontological sense) to its real self.  Hegel's Phenomenology must not only be interpreted as the statement of a system but as a path of consciousness to absolute knowledge which is conditioned by the absolute.  In a certain sense Hegel repeats the dichotomy of the formula of ''metaphysical perception''.
RP SOBOTKA, M (reprint author), UNIV KARLOVA,FILOS FAK,PRAGUE,CZECH REPUBLIC.

AB Is consciousness or the subject part of the natural world or the human world? Can we write intentionality, so central in Husserl's philosophy, into Quine's system of ontological naturalism and naturalized epistemology - or into Heidegger's account of human being and existential phenomenology? PT J
AU KUNG, G
AF KUNG, G
TI ONTOLOGY AND THE CONSTRUCTION OF SYSTEMS
SO SYNTHESE
LA English
DT Article; Proceedings Paper
CT AUTHORS COLLOQUIUM WITH NELSON GOODMAN AND CATHERINE Z ELGIN
CY MAR 18-20, 1991
CL UNIV BIELEFELD, ZENTRUM INTERDIZIPLINARE FORSCH, BIELEFELD, GERMANY
HO UNIV BIELEFELD, ZENTRUM INTERDIZIPLINARE FORSCH
AB After drawing attention to the basic importance of Goodman's work The Structure of Appearance, this paper turns to a critical analysis of Goodman's claims concerning worldmaking. It stresses that Goodman's acceptance of a multiplicity of actual worlds does not involve the belief in an unknowable underlying reality; but that it is due to the non-mysterious fact that constructional systems allow for a multiplicity of disagreeing, right versions. However, from the point of view of truthmaker ontology, most 'worlds' of constructional systems are not genuine worlds; and so far it has not been shown that there are genuine truthmaker worlds that disagree.
   It is suggested that the construction of systems usually involves three conflicting aims: the logical, the ontological, and the psychological. Considering the current interest in cognitive psychology and phenomenology, the implications of the psychological aim, too, deserve to be reexamined.
RP KUNG, G (reprint author), UNIV FRIBOURG,PHILOSOPHISCH SEMINAR,CH-1700 FRIBOURG,SWITZERLAND.

PT J
AU JAMME, C
AF JAMME, C
TI THE CONCEPT OF MYTH IN HEGEL EARLY WRITINGS (1787-1807)
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB This article is the text of a lecture which the author delivered in autumn 1990 in Prague. At that time he rested on his discussion of The Concept of Myth in Hegel's Early Writings published in the collection The Dawn of Idealism and the Dawn of the Romantic.
   In the first part the author outlines Hegel's concept of myth in the period of Early Theological Writings and tries to ascertain the influences with which Hegel was coping at that time. In contrast with the typical enlightenment concepts (Mendelssohn, Kant), which distinguished enlightenment for the educated and enlightenment for the people, Hegel aligned himself with the Herderian ''religious fantasy'' which links the idea of beauty with the moral self-realisation of the nation. Hegel therefore intended to create a new mythology, which was to have a revolutionising impact on the German state of mind. Apart from Herder's influence, the author stresses the special significance of Rousseau for Hegel and for the development of his concept of myth; as well as the deep-reaching effect which Gibbon had on Hegel. Kant, on the other hand, inspired his idea of the morality of reason, which should be sensorily symbolised in myth.
   In the second section the author considered Hegel's own revision of his aesthetic views in Faith and Knowledge and in Theses on Difference. Unlike Kant, Hegel insisted on the unity of beauty and right and shared Schelling's concept of philosophy and art as equal realms of right. Later however his ideas changed. Philosophy came to have precedence over art and nature as a whole could be understood only philosophically. Finally the adult Hegel, in the fragments that Hoffmeister named The Spirit of the Middle Ages, came to the idea that history can be understood only as a conception. Thus philosophy was in the end overtaken by art.
   In the conclusion to the article the author discusses the art of religion in Hegel's Phenomenology of the Spirit. He shows that by this time Hegel no longer shared the conviction of the superiority of Greek mythology over christianity. The Greek myths were imperfect because they presented gods in human bodies. For this reason they could not exist historically. In the Berlin Lectures on Aesthetics there are no longer changes in Hegel on this point.

PT J
AU STEINER, P
AF STEINER, P
TI SHPET,GUSTAV AND THE PRAGUE SCHOOL - CONCEPTUAL FRAMES FOR THE STUDY OF
   LANGUAGE
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB In this study, which returns to the theoretical foundations of structuralism, the author compares an approach to language such as the semiotic system of the Russian philosopher Gustav Shpet with the concepts of the Prague school. He shows that, like the Czech linguists, Shpet rejected the strict Saussurean dichotomy of langue-parole and this brought him closer to the Prague Linguistic Circle and its functionalist understanding of speech. In his investigations of speech from the point of view of its entelechy, however, Shpet insists on the predominant position of the ''identifying'' function of speech. If Prague structuralism is closely linked with phenomenology, Shpet's philosophy of language can be labelled a phenomenological branch of structuralism.
RP STEINER, P (reprint author), UNIV PENN,PHILADELPHIA,PA 19104, USA.

PT J
AU SCHALLER, K
AF SCHALLER, K
TI COMENIUS AND THE OPEN SOUL - PATOCKA INTERPRETATION OF COMENIUS AND ITS
   MEANING FOR CONTEMPORARY SYSTEMATIC PEDAGOGY
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The Czech philosopher Jan Patocka interpreted Comenius from the position of phenomenology, of which he was a noted representative.  For him, Comenius's thoughts were not stimulating for their apparent relation to modern European rationalism, as others have tried to show, but because they discuss the borders of our habitual spiritual universe.  For Patocka, the key points of Comenius's pan-sophism are:  non-Cartesian rationality; a non-subjective and eccentric (non-egological and decentralized) conception of the subject:  the open soul and its anchor in the natural world; and the human being as a task to be realized.
   Patocka sees that the end of the epoch of closed reserve is coming.  The new age calls for a pedagogical turn, in no way continuing mere teachings about the pre-formed person, but one that opens him to everything he sets his sights on and devotes himself to.  It calls for a pedagogy that understands that the soul has its center outside of things - - consequently outside of itself - and its mission is to continue to devote itself.  Patocka showed that we must read Comenius differently today, sometimes even against him himself, because the rational school system he drew up - which should be the vehicle for the teachings of true humanity - increasingly from the era of the enlightenment became reduced to professional preparation, the complete antagonist of Comenius's understanding of true education.  By his stimulus, Patocka influenced an entire generation of his contemporaries and successors attempting to orient pedagogy towards an agenda.
RP SCHALLER, K (reprint author), RUHR UNIV BOCHUM,W-4630 BOCHUM,GERMANY.

AU ADRIAANSE, HJ
AF ADRIAANSE, HJ
TI HUSSERL AS A PHILOSOPHER OF CULTURE ON THE XXVII VOLUME HUSSERLIANA
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The lecture Husserl as a Philosopher of Culture was delivered at the Institute for Philosophy of the Czecho-Slovak Academy of Sciences on the 28th of May, 1991. It is devoted to the XXVII. Volume Husserliana.  The author's main aim is to show Husserl's ethico-normative conception of culture in its relationship to individual and social ethics as well as its relationship to science and philosophy, and as a basis for the analysis of Husserl's texts from the 1920s on the position, sense and renewal of contemporary culture (articles for Kaizo). The mediating element here is Husserl's conviction that the responsible sense-clarification in the sphere of culture is in the last instance possible only on the basis of transcendental phenomenology.

PT J
AU CIBULKA, J
AF CIBULKA, J
TI THE CONCEPTION OF HERMENEUTICS OF FACTICITY IN THE EARLY HEIDEGGER WORKS
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB Heidegger's text on "facticity hermeneutics" of 1923 (Vol. 63 G A) defines the early Heidegger's attitude towards those philosophical trends which acted in Germany at the beginning of the twenties. He polemizes particularly against the anti-Kantian trend of the ontologism of "things in themselves" of that time.
   Husserl's phenomenological philosophy did not reject under the slogan "to the things themselves" Kant's asking for the transcendental presuppositions of cognition, in rather incarnated them in the phenomenological view itself.
   Jacques Derrida shows that Heidegger builds philosophically on the experience of questioning. The freedom of asking is not preceded by anything, the leap of the question liberates the beginning. Heidegger does not leap over the dimension of Kantian transcendental presuppositions of cognition but he renews it by means of its inner self-destruction.
   Heidegger's "Dasein" is in Walter Schulze's terminology a "being subject", a privileged facticity which arranges ontically itself and the remaining facticity. Heidegger tries to retain possibility as possibility.
   It is interpretativeness which penetrates the facticity of life against Dilthey's "rawness" of actual life. In spite of this fact facticity consumes itself, it is even already destroyed when it becomes the theme of our philosophical interpretation.
   In Heidegger's opinion, significance is not an object definition but it characterizes our procuring meeting.
   The authentic "Dasein" puts on the mask of averaging anonymity of the word "man". Van Gogh who regarded his "Dasein" without this mask became insane.
   A really evident phenomenal ground is provided for ontological possibility only by the actual ontical experience which "testifies" to the actual possibility of the ontological idea of this possibility.
   Heideggeer's text Hermeneutics of Facticity also contains a supplement entitled Dialectics and Phenomenology. It defines as the superiority of "dialectics" against phenomenology the fact that dialectics reflects phenomenology as a level of immediacy of comprehension and that "dialectics" succeeds in penetrating the terrain of irrationality in an ever deeper way. Heidegger criticizes "dialectics" owing to the fact that it does not recognize from the object itself in what way the object should be experienced and what are the limits of the optics of this experience.
   A higher level of the dialogue between Heidegger's hermeneutic philosophy and dialectics is represented by Gadamees five hermeneutic studies Hegel's Dialectics. In Hegel's early theological contemplation it is the moment of the topicality of dialectical revolution of the pole of the good in the pole of evil which is philosophically of the greatest importance.
RP CIBULKA, J (reprint author), SLOVAK ACAD SCI,FILOZOF USTAV,CS-80936 BRATISLAVA,CZECHOSLOVAKIA.

PT J
AU KOTATKO, P
AF KOTATKO, P
TI WITTGENSTEIN AND THE CARTESIAN SUBJECT
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
AB The paper deals with two Wittgenstein's arguments concerning the problem of rule-following: the argument against private language (PIA), i.e. language whose expressions refer or apply exclusively to private phenomena, and the argument against individual language (ILA), i.e. language whose semantic rules are introduced and fixed by intentions of on isolated speaker. Part I suggests a way of reconstruction of PLA, while part II characterizes Wittgenstein's account of public discourse about mental states in the light of PLA. Parts III-V discuss ILA, esp. in its Kripkean version, focussing on its consequences for the account of public communication. The idea of the general agreement in communicative practices (based on the shared form of life) and of rule-following as a participation in these practices, is liable to the same kind of scepticism as IL. To be immune, it requires a weaker notion of rules than that exploited in the sceptical part of ILA. ILA (and PLA) is a good introduction to the externalist account of meaning and communication, but is not sufficient to refute internalism. On the other side, ILA und PLA challenge the Cartesian notion of subject as a realm of transparency and absolute evidences: hence they undermine ambitions of philosophical programmes like Husserl's transcendental phenomenology, independently on whether these presuppose the possibility of IL (PL) or not (part VIII).

CT 18TH WORLD CONGRESS OF PHILOSOPHY

CL BRIGHTON, ENGLAND
AB A phenomenology of action is outlined, analyzing the structure of volition, kinesthesis, and perception in the experience of action, and finally, the experience of embodiment in action. PT J
AU SIEMEK, MJ
AF SIEMEK, MJ
TI POLAND - PHILOSOPHY AND SOCIETY
SO STUDIES IN SOVIET THOUGHT
LA German
DT Article
AB In the former socialist countries the relation of philosophy to social reality, as shaped by the political interests of the State, must be considered for each particular case with a view to the historical dynamics of its own development. The Polish case is not typical in this regard - it was determined by the failure of forced sovietization at the institutional, cultural level and the maintenance of Poland's traditional contacts with Western European culture. In this regard Polish universities played an important role since they preserved 'normal' structures of academic activity.
   Philosophy in Poland has been marked by three currents which managed to rebuff the advances of Marxism. Best known among them is the Lwow-Warsaw school of logic and analytic philosophy. Its contribution is especially important in the methodology of philosophical inquiry and the style of responsible philosophical discourse. Though not a bed of political opposition the school's members mounted a 'spiritual opposition' to Marxism, especially during the Solidarity period. Catholic philosophy, as represented by the Catholic University of Lublin (KUL), has been and remains a completely independent force in Eastern and Central Europe. Doctrinally, Catholic philosophy in Poland has been divided between a more traditional, dated Thomism and the more progressive circles in Cracow where Western European philosophy, especially phenomenology, existentialism, and hermeneutics, has been influential (the significance of Jozef Tischner and Karol Wojtyla, now Pope Jean Paul II). The third major current, centered in Cracow, is the phenomenology of Roman Ingarden, whose influence is manifest in a style of philosophizing that has attracted professional philosophers as well as committed intellectuals from different disciplines.
   Against this diversified background Marxism had to prove its mettle, a requirement that explains the unique internal differentiation and pluralism within Polish Marxist philosophy. The decisive factors in its development were its rejection of Soviet Marxism-Leninism and the opening to Western Marxism. The often remarked 'revisionist' character of Polish Marxism consists in the recognition by its most talented representatives of the fundamental incompatibility between Marxism as the ideological legitimation of the Communist state and as a philosophy subject to rational criteria. In effect, Polish Marxists thought the theory through to its end, and in this sense brought to light its inherently unstable nature: to be a philosophy it would have to cease to be Marxist, to be Marxist means having to pander to ideology.
   The confrontation of the diverse currents in Polish philosophy contributed to an unfortunate result: for political reasons non-Marxists steered clear of social philosophical questions, while official Marxism, which suppressed the 'revisionist' trend, obfuscated the relation between philosophy and social reality. Today philosophy in Poland has the urgent task to contribute to the reconstruction of a social rationality combining a critical consciousness with moral elements of commitment and responsibility.
RP SIEMEK, MJ (reprint author), UNIV WARSAW,INST PHILOSOPHY & SOCIOL,PL-00325 WARSAW,POLAND.

PT J
AU SVOBODOVA, H
AF SVOBODOVA, H
TI NATURE - LANDSCAPE - HUMAN-BEING (CHAPTERS ON SOCIOLOGICAL-THEORY OF
   LANDSCAPE)
SO FILOSOFICKY CASOPIS
LA Czech
DT Article
DE SOCIOLOGY OF LANDSCAPE; SOCIOLOGY OF CULTURE; HUMAN ECOLOGY;
   ENVIRONMENTAL PSYCHOLOGY; ENVIRONMENTAL AESTHETICS; PHENOMENOLOGY;
   PRACTICAL APPROACHES TO SPATIAL SCIENCES (PLACE, SPACE, ENVIRONMENT,
   LANDSCAPE, SETTLEMENT, DWELLING)
AB Concerning the theme of the ethical approaches to human being to environment and landscape this paper will attempt to formulate four hypotheses.  They can help toward a practical solution involving the ethical complexities in the relationship between present and future generations with landscape.  From a geographical and sociological point of view the problems that face Western Europe and Eastern and Central Europe are different.  The four hypotheses are founded on the theme concerning the relation between man and unspoilt nature and landscape:  1.  The human being as individual 2.  The human being as part of a microsocial structure 3.  The human being as part of a macrosocial structure 4.  The temporary differences between Western and Eastern Europe in their current processes of development.
   Present and future approaches of human beings to the environment and the landscape can be observed in contemporary so called postmodernist art and culture.  The term postmodernism covers not only art and literature, but a very broad field of human culture as well.  The theme of nature and landscape originally occurred in some environmental sculptures.  It helps with the growth of ecological sensibility through technological modernisation in architecture, desing and art.  The contemporary chaos in society is reflected deeply in the effects it has on the cultural landscape and in the cultural behaviour of human beings towards nature.
